<div id="review_2085366423" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2085366423" />
    <a title="Allison Hurd" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14495279-allison-hurd"><img alt="Allison Hurd" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530670583p2/14495279.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2085366423?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Allison Hurd" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Allison Hurd" href="/user/show/14495279-allison-hurd">Allison Hurd</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14495279-allison-hurd?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14495279-allison-hurd?shelf=fem-author">fem-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2085366423" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4472123283374766608">What a strange book. I love Valente's silvery, magical prose, and the way she weaves the everyday with the bizarre in a way that makes all the bizarre things seem commonplace and the everyday things seem electric. But even with all that, this book felt a bit more like someone retelling you their dream upon waking than it felt like crossing viscerally into that dream.<br/><br/><b>CONTENT WARNING</b>: <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[ loss of a loved one, incest, adultery, body horror/mutilation, mental illness, suicide, graphic se</span></span>
  <span id="freeText4472123283374766608" style="display:none">What a strange book. I love Valente's silvery, magical prose, and the way she weaves the everyday with the bizarre in a way that makes all the bizarre things seem commonplace and the everyday things seem electric. But even with all that, this book felt a bit more like someone retelling you their dream upon waking than it felt like crossing viscerally into that dream.<br /><br /><b>CONTENT WARNING</b>: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ loss of a loved one, incest, adultery, body horror/mutilation, mental illness, suicide, graphic sex--sometimes somewhat rapey or uncomfortable.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><u>Things that were lovely:</u><br /><br />-<b>The writing</b>. It would almost be purple if it wasn't so careful. You can feel her love of words, reminiscent of Mieville's.<br /><br />-<b>The concepts</b>. I dare you to find another book with these ideas or construction. A sexually transmitted city? How do you come up with this stuff, Cat??<br /><br />-<b>The characters</b>. Valente doesn't much care about the physical appearance of her characters. When she describes them, she gifts readers with their most vulnerable, personal inside worlds. And, like all trust freely given, it forms a bond between you and them.<br /><br />-<b>The wordplay</b>. I don't want to give it away, but there are a lot of puns and folktale-esque interpretations of things that are little bursts of joy.<br /><br /><u>Things I did not love:</u><br /><br />-<b>The sex</b>. Yes. I got it. In order for people of our world to get to Palimpsest, they've gotta get it on. That wasn't the interesting part to me. And, if we ARE going to focus on the mechanics of it, then sorry, you gotta actually have mechanics. How does the city know which part to take them to? What's the minimum sexual contact required to get there? This was wishy-washy, and didn't heighten anything for me. The descriptions were not meant to titillate, they were very business-like, but I couldn't understand the transaction. Not sure why we watched the haggling rather than focusing on the good that is desired. Also, there were parts that were...uncomfortable, with people who seemed young or unwilling or various other things that made it a bizarre focal point.<br /><br />-<b>The why of it</b>. We sort of get it, but not really, and as that's the last part of the book, I was left wanting.<br /><br />-<b>The pacing</b>. We spend a looooot of time in the grotesqueness of needing to bed strangers to live the life you can't escape than we do on any other part of the story. Everything else is handed to the characters in a way that made me feel like the people whose heads I was occupying weren't actually the important heads. I hate getting the story sideways. Stories should be from the POV of the person moving the story forward, in my opinion, otherwise I feel robbed of the glory of the pivotal moments. And then the end was quick. <br /><br />I have a lot of questions and I'm not sure there are answers for them, and if there were, it would likely involve clinical descriptions of various sexual positions and things like mucus levels or sperm count, so I'm just going to leave them unasked. It was...a novel, trippy experience. She's certainly imaginative, but this did not coalesce as brilliantly as I would have hoped. <br /><br />2.5 stars rounded up because I did get some entertainment out of it throughout the book. Although, looking at my read dates, this took me 2 weeks to complete and it's not that long. Well, whatever. I shall round up, and hope my next by her is more to my liking.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4472123283374766608" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1422639707" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1422639707" />
    <a title="Gabrielle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle"><img alt="Gabrielle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598976963p2/6753400.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1422639707?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gabrielle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gabrielle" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle">Gabrielle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=weird">weird</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=read-in-2017">read-in-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=erotica">erotica</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=steampunk">steampunk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=own-a-copy">own-a-copy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1422639707" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17069572420778903998">3 and a half stars, rounded to 4.<br/><br/>The first thing you need to understand when you crack open a Catherynne M. Valente book is that she is a poet who writes novels. Her sentences and descriptions are dreamlike, her words carefully chosen but often surprising. I would personally like to find a good seat in that woman's brain and just watch her thoughts fly by because the images she conjures are often some of the most beautiful ones I have ever read.<br/><br/>A criticism I have often read about her is that the</span>
  <span id="freeText17069572420778903998" style="display:none">3 and a half stars, rounded to 4.<br /><br />The first thing you need to understand when you crack open a Catherynne M. Valente book is that she is a poet who writes novels. Her sentences and descriptions are dreamlike, her words carefully chosen but often surprising. I would personally like to find a good seat in that woman's brain and just watch her thoughts fly by because the images she conjures are often some of the most beautiful ones I have ever read.<br /><br />A criticism I have often read about her is that there is a lot of style over substance. And honestly, that does happen. When you get lost in the beauty of her language, her plots can sometimes get a little confusing. But to me that's a minor irritant because I just love to bask in the whimsical beauty of whatever the Hell she feels like writing.<br /><br />I put this book on my "erotica" shelf, but there is a lot more to it than sex. In fact, the sex quickly becomes a means to an end and is not the focus of the story very long. Because this story is about cities, about our inner worlds and how beautiful and guarded those secret place within ourselves are, and how hard it is to access them and truly understand them. Yes, there is a lot of sex in this book, but I found it made me ponder the nature of intimacy much more than carnality.<br /><br />Palimpsest is a sexually transmitted city: only by sleeping with people who have already been there, and wear its mark on their skin, can you access it, and once you have, the city will obsesses you and you will only care about going back. People who have visited Palimpsest can recognize each other: something in their body language gives them away just as much as the partial map of the city that appears on their skin, like a tattoo or a scar. The book follows the journey of four such visitors: a Japanese train enthusiast, an American beekeeper, a Russian locksmith and an Italian bookbinder.<br /><br />The book is composed of vignettes of our four characters in our world, and their attempts to go back to Palimpsest, and then what happens to them when they are in the elusive city. Everything is beautifully written, but to be honest, nothing much happens... The quarto is finally given a sort-of goal about halfway through the book, but even then, things move at a snail's pace.<br /><br />My husband had an interesting theory about the way one's first visit to Palimpsest and getting the strange map tattoo could be a metaphor about losing one's virginity and how being sexually active affects one's view of the world, how it influences interactions with people (who are now also potential partners) and changes one's behavior. He has a point: sex does change a person, and not just the first time. Various experiences do leave their mark, whether on the body or on the mind: it can be a disease or a pregnancy, but it can also be a cherished memory or a regret.<br /><br />The story could also be taken to be about being haunted by a place, a memory that left a really strong mark on one's soul and the fruitless quest to recapture it. I am noticing a common theme favored by the New Weird writers: Vandermeer has Ambergris, Mieville has New Crobuzon... All these imaginary urban landscapes are fascinating, baroque and beautiful creations, and now I feel like I should read "Invisible Cities" and see where their inspiration seems to have come from.<br /><br />I am rating it 3 and a half stars rounded up to 4 because really, Valente's prose is just so freaking beautiful that I can't find it in me to round it down. That being said, "Deathless" and "Radiance" had actual plots, which made them much more enjoyable reads (for my structured mind, anyway). "Palimpsest" is a gorgeous but ultimately frustrating read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17069572420778903998" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3642035582201028170">Anything I say about this book just will not do it justice. It can't describe how I found myself holding my breath at writing that felt so intimate, almost like I shouldn't be reading it. Completely immersive and beautiful and disturbing and I can't wait to go back and read everything else she has written.<br/><br/>Kind of like if Jeanette Winterson and Neil Gaiman wrote a book together, after reading The Pillow Book. <br/><br/>I hadn't heard of Valente yet, and only read her because this book was nominated for the</span>
  <span id="freeText3642035582201028170" style="display:none">Anything I say about this book just will not do it justice. It can't describe how I found myself holding my breath at writing that felt so intimate, almost like I shouldn't be reading it. Completely immersive and beautiful and disturbing and I can't wait to go back and read everything else she has written.<br /><br />Kind of like if Jeanette Winterson and Neil Gaiman wrote a book together, after reading The Pillow Book. <br /><br />I hadn't heard of Valente yet, and only read her because this book was nominated for the Hugo for 2010. I hope it wins, definitely my favorite of the six.</span>

      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review109576217">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3347928876" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3347928876" />
    <a title="Jennifer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1188456-jennifer"><img alt="Jennifer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1482893253p2/1188456.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3347928876?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer" href="/user/show/1188456-jennifer">Jennifer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3347928876" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6904830637375731156">Catherynne Valente writes somewhere at the crossroads of Italo Calvino (dense, erudite, inexplicable), <i>The Travels of Marco Polo</i> (bizarre travelogue with little commentary on the weirdness), and fever dream. <b>Palimpsest</b> leans hard in the fever dream direction. This is a city of dreams and desire, transmitted like a sexual infection: those who have slept with a traveler to Palimpsest wake up with a sprawling tattoo of a map somewhere on their body, and the next person to sleep with them will be tr</span>
  <span id="freeText6904830637375731156" style="display:none">Catherynne Valente writes somewhere at the crossroads of Italo Calvino (dense, erudite, inexplicable), <i>The Travels of Marco Polo</i> (bizarre travelogue with little commentary on the weirdness), and fever dream. <b>Palimpsest</b> leans hard in the fever dream direction. This is a city of dreams and desire, transmitted like a sexual infection: those who have slept with a traveler to Palimpsest wake up with a sprawling tattoo of a map somewhere on their body, and the next person to sleep with them will be transported to that particular part of Palimpsest. But they can only stay while they are asleep, and they cannot see more of Palimpsest beyond the area tattooed upon their lover. Almost all awaken with a burning desire to return to the city, which promises all the things they have yearned for in their waking lives. <br /><br />This is, no doubt, the weirdest portal fantasy I've ever read, weird in a way that only Valente could do. It's also a hard book to read: unlike <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2887202235" rel="nofollow noopener">The Orphan's Tales</a>, which enchanted me from start to finish, <b>Palimpsest</b> walks the line between revulsion and intrigue, a push-pull interaction with the reader that makes for a very uncomfortable reading experience. I would put it down and pick it up again five minutes later in spite of myself.<br /><br />Push: the only way into Palimpsest is sex with a fellow tattooed traveler. The need to return to Palimpsest demands both quantity and diversity of partners, but for all that the four central characters are almost consumed whole by their desires - for a drowned sister, a lost wife, direction, belonging - this is a book of sex devoid of sexual desire. Sex is a transaction, a utilitarian, ugly thing that uses other people as doorways. Attraction, respect, affection, and pleasure play no role: it's simply fucking. Our characters, spread between Kyoto, New York City, California, and Rome seek out tattooed strangers with the same undiscriminating desperation that junkies seek out dealers. It's bald and horrible; they feel it, and they suffer intensely while pursuing their shared obsession. One character walks shirtless through the streets of New York City in the winter for weeks to advertise his tattooed belly, hoping to attract another Palimpsest traveler.<br /><br />Pull: no one writes fantasy worlds like Catherynne Valente. Her head is an exceedingly strange place, and surely that's one of the great pleasures of books - to take you inside the weirdness of other people's heads. She writes poetry the way the rest of us breathe, and she is at her finest when describing the minute, sensuous details that make up her city:<br /><br /><blockquote>He is a breeder of snails and dwells in a house of three stories. In his youth he covered each of the floors in rich soils of black and red, leaves of gold and green, grains brown and sweet, violet petals as thick as a pat of butter. In his middle age, birch and fig saplings sprouted through the kitchen tile; hedges ring the furniture. His great petit-gris slowly move from parlor to wash-closet, gorging themselves in paroxysms of helical rapture. They mate in the chimney, sing softly the hymns of snails on the windowsills when the moon blanches the verdant paint to black. </blockquote><br /><br />And<blockquote>November veils herself and takes the finch by its roasted beak, pushing it into her mouth with two fingers, her remaining blessings. It is sweet, at first, the burnished skin and meat, glazed in something like brandy and something like plum wine. But as she chews - methodically, for it fills her mouth to bursting - the organs rupture, bitter and bilious, a taste like despair, like the loss of love. And deeper, the bones shiver and crack and cut her - the taste of her blood flows in, salty as tears shed over a ruined body, mingling with the marrow, and it is sweet again, sweet as herself, herself remaining at the end of all trials. And November can see why the veil is needed. No god should bear witness to a woman devouring a meal of herself.</blockquote><br /><br />Within Palimpsest are trains like biological organisms, a homunculus made of bees, blank children who are licked into shape and life, intricate tea rituals, old wars, mute people with transplanted animal limbs and heads. And beyond these wonders are old scars, failed relationships, hallucinations; the luggage that travelers can't shed, even in Palimpsest.<br /><br />This is my fourth book by Valente, and while the intensity with which her characters desire is constant throughout her work, they seem to suffer the most for it in this book. Still, I was prepared to give it four stars as the threads of the four characters start to weave together and Palimpsest relinquishes some of its mysteries. Unfortunately, I didn't like the ending - the desires that have flagellated our four characters this whole time go out with a whimper of satiation, and this seems pat, easy, and wrong; an ending that doesn't suit the complex and often sinister role that Palimpsest has played.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6904830637375731156" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/872670301" />
    <a title="Megan Baxter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5742086-megan-baxter"><img alt="Megan Baxter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1310153693p2/5742086.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/872670301?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Megan Baxter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Megan Baxter" href="/user/show/5742086-megan-baxter">Megan Baxter</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11928476771946139983">It's odd that, in trying to figure out how to explain this book, I first have to figure out exactly what is the sexually transmitted disease. It's not citizenship in the strange city of Palimpsest - that has to be earned. It's not passage to the city, as that has to be achieved, every time someone goes. It's the passport, I guess. The black markings of the city streets on the skin that never leave, that mark a person as someone who could go to Palimpsest, if they choose. Tattoo as sexually trans</span>
  <span id="freeText11928476771946139983" style="display:none">It's odd that, in trying to figure out how to explain this book, I first have to figure out exactly what is the sexually transmitted disease. It's not citizenship in the strange city of Palimpsest - that has to be earned. It's not passage to the city, as that has to be achieved, every time someone goes. It's the passport, I guess. The black markings of the city streets on the skin that never leave, that mark a person as someone who could go to Palimpsest, if they choose. Tattoo as sexually transmitted disease (okay, fine: infection. I know it's supposed to be infection these days. I just grew up with STD as the acronym and am having trouble switching to STI.)<br /> <br />Note: The rest of this review has been withdrawn due to the changes in Goodreads policy and enforcement. <div id="review_2481049101" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2481049101" />
    <a title="Acqua" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47160872-acqua"><img alt="Acqua" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548417878p2/47160872.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2481049101?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Acqua" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Acqua" href="/user/show/47160872-acqua">Acqua</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47160872-acqua?shelf=w-ones">w-ones</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47160872-acqua?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47160872-acqua?shelf=db">db</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47160872-acqua?shelf=love-polygons">love-polygons</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47160872-acqua?shelf=loved-it">loved-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47160872-acqua?shelf=polyam-main">polyam-main</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47160872-acqua?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47160872-acqua?shelf=q-allcast">q-allcast</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47160872-acqua?shelf=qm">qm</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47160872-acqua?shelf=qf">qf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2481049101" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9017126291970544936">3.5 stars, I think.<br/><b><i>Palimpsest</i> is a story about a sexually transmitted city</b>.<br/>It's strange and uncomfortable; it has an idea and goes for it without ever trying to hide that, without compromises, which is something I always appreciate. It could be seen as <b>a response to the Greek myth that says people are only half of a whole, or the soulmate trope in general</b>: it asks <i>why does it have to be two</i>? As I'm someone who loves stories about cities and human sexuality but not about soulmates, I really liked</span>
  <span id="freeText9017126291970544936" style="display:none">3.5 stars, I think.<br /><b><i>Palimpsest</i> is a story about a sexually transmitted city</b>.<br />It's strange and uncomfortable; it has an idea and goes for it without ever trying to hide that, without compromises, which is something I always appreciate. It could be seen as <b>a response to the Greek myth that says people are only half of a whole, or the soulmate trope in general</b>: it asks <i>why does it have to be two</i>? As I'm someone who loves stories about cities and human sexuality but not about soulmates, I really liked what this book did.<br />However, I'm still really conflicted about it, because it got as many things wrong as it got right.<br /><br />Some have said that this book was <b>"ahead of its time", and in some ways, it is</b>. How many books with an all-queer, polyamorous cast and a happy ending are there? Not many, and it's 2020. This was published in 2009, and I think the author received homophobic and biphobic harassment when it got more visibility due to an award nomination.<br />In other ways, <i>Palimpsest</i> is very much a book published in 2009, and I don't mean that because of certain tropes, but because of how much of <b>an unintentional-yet-proud display of American ignorance and self-centeredness it is</b>.<br /><br />Valente having misrepresented other cultures is not news in the slightest, but in <i>Palimpsest</i> you get to witness how she writes about the American character in comparison to the other three, non-American PoVs, and the difference is stark. <b>While the American character gets to be a character, the others are written as if their culture were a personality trait.</b><br /><br />The Italian character feels like a cobbled-up caricature of things taken from various parts of Italian history, and <i>of course</i> he's passionate and sexist, <b>of course he doesn't know English, and going from how this book writes the parts in Italian, he can't speak Italian either</b>.<br />The Russian character's whole personality is "sad"; the Japanese girl has blue hair (of course) and the way she was written gave me a weird feeling in a bad way; however I'm neither Russian nor Japanese and I can't play the game of "spot the gross stereotype" as well as I could with Ludovico. And Ludovico's PoV was a minefield of ignorance. I can forgive it a little more since <b>conversations around representation and US-centrism weren't as easily accessible and easily found then</b> as they are today (in which there'd really be no excuse for writing a book like this and yet it keeps happening), but still.<br /><br />And can we talk about the weird/unintentionally comedic effect of naming an Italian character "Nerezza"? It's an attempt at a translation of "blackness" (Ebony Dark'ness Dementia Raven Way, is that you?) but sounds like an insult due to the assonance with other words with a -zz sound: "schifezza", grossness, "ribrezzo", instinctive disgust, and since she's from Rome, "monnezza", garbage in Romanesco. <b>Nerezza monnezza, the poor woman would have been bullied so much.</b><br />(If you're going to make up names in a language you don't know, please ask a native speaker to make sure they don't rhyme with several insults.)<br /><br />But it gets worse! In the scene in which the American woman, November, first talks on the phone with Ludovico, she realizes that he only knows very basic English, and he is embarrassed and apologizes profusely. When they meet, <i>in Italy</i>, one of the first things November mentions is his "gnarled and barbed" accent while speaking in English (of course).<br /><b>It never occurs to her to learn one word in Italian!</b> It never occurs to her to apologize, or to be embarrassed for her ignorance; it certainly never occurs to the author either, and given what she has written, she should be. "<i>Oh Christo</i>", you say? I'm cringing into the next century.<br /><br />And I'm annoyed because I know what this could have been had the author not used cultures just for the exotic flavor, had she actually done her research, had she actually asked people from said cultures to read what she wrote. <b>The only fact that I did finish this book should tell you how good it was otherwise</b>.<br /><br />This is a story about four people who first find each other in the dream city of Palimpsest, and then have to find each other again outside of it to stay in it: here, they can be who they really are in a way they never could in the real world. It's a story as full of yearning as it is of uncomfortable sex scenes, and it's at its heart about <b>the changing norms, the hidden signals and the gatekeeping of marginalized communities</b> (the Nerezza plot point: it will never be easy for me, so why it should be for you? So much pain from that).<br /><br />And Palimpsest is wonderful to read about! A magical, terrifying city that feels so freeing and yet is all but an utopia, with its own issues and disparities and <b>people who try to cling to the past when that's not possible, not when cities are always rewriting themselves</b>.<br />I also love the writing. One could say it's too much, but I don't mind, I like books written in an unconventional way, in which the writing almost feels like a character in itself, in which you won't forget it easily.<br /><br />This is not a nice story; it's messy, it focuses on all kinds of outcasts and certainly has teeth, in a way that makes me think it wouldn't fit in even if it was published today after a good round of culture-focused editing - because today's major issue in the queer book community isn't so much the queerphobia, but the idea that portraying something uncomfortable must be an endorsement of it, and this book has <i>so much</i> of that kind of content. <b>It has at least two plot points revolving around fraternal incest; it would need its own magical Palimpsest publishing industry to exist as it is</b> without facing significant backlash.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9017126291970544936" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_66168352" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/66168352" />
    <a title="Stefan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2167401-stefan"><img alt="Stefan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1238189504p2/2167401.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/66168352?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stefan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stefan" href="/user/show/2167401-stefan">Stefan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Stefan by: </span>
      Camille
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2167401-stefan?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2167401-stefan?shelf=library-loan">library-loan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer66168352" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14666903262404278959">The first thing that strikes you about this book is the gorgeous prose. Every sentence is crafted with the utmost care, resulting in a novel that almost reads like poetry. It simply begs to be read out loud. I've read many books that attempt this kind of lush prose, but Palimpsest is one of the most successful and most beautiful.<br/><br/>Palimpsest is a sexually transmitted city. People who have been there have a small tattoo - a piece of the city's map - somewhere on their body. Sleep with them and you </span>
  <span id="freeText14666903262404278959" style="display:none">The first thing that strikes you about this book is the gorgeous prose. Every sentence is crafted with the utmost care, resulting in a novel that almost reads like poetry. It simply begs to be read out loud. I've read many books that attempt this kind of lush prose, but Palimpsest is one of the most successful and most beautiful.<br /><br />Palimpsest is a sexually transmitted city. People who have been there have a small tattoo - a piece of the city's map - somewhere on their body. Sleep with them and you are transported there. When you wake up, back in the real world, you will find a small tattoo of another part of Palimpsest on your body - and you will want to go back.<br /><br />The story follows four people who are all newcomers to Palimpsest - a young Japanese woman, a beekeeper, a locksmith, a bookbinder. They all have lost something in the real world and are naturally drawn to Palimpsest. As the story progresses, more and more details about their lives, and about the strange city of Palimpsest, are revealed. While the novel, at first, seems like four more or less independent stories, told in alternating chapters, slowly but surely a plot develops that connects everything and leads to a beautiful, bittersweet conclusion.<br /><br /><br />An interesting aspect of the novel is its strained eroticism. After the initial "connections" that introduce the four protagonists to Palimpsest, they find themselves wanting to return, which can only be done by sleeping with another "infected" person. The resulting scenes are almost uncomfortable to read - while they're at times fairly explicit, the sex is mainly a mode of transportation, something you have to get through. <br /><br />This is a novel to read slowly and savor, because it'll just be over all too soon. I found myself rereading entire chapters after turning the last page. I would recommend this without hesitation to fans of China Mieville, but also to anyone else who appreciates a slow-moving, lyrical and entirely unique story. Absolutely gorgeous.<br /><br />(If you'd like to check out a sample, here's a link to the short story "Palimpsest": <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.sensesfive.com/samples/palimpsest.php">http://www.sensesfive.com/samples/pal...</a> )</span>
  <a data-text-id="14666903262404278959" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer48310847" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16208943969716371675">This is urban fantasy where the main character is a "fantasy" city. You can't get to Palimpsest unless you've slept with someone who's been there. You can't get to any other parts of the city unless you sleep with someone else.<br/><br/>It's an intoxicating read. For the continuing presence of sex in the narrative, this is not a romance. It's mentioned, even described, but it's a vehicle by which the human characters are able to find their way around a city where they seem to be meant to be.<br/><br/>Like The Orpha</span>
  <span id="freeText16208943969716371675" style="display:none">This is urban fantasy where the main character is a "fantasy" city. You can't get to Palimpsest unless you've slept with someone who's been there. You can't get to any other parts of the city unless you sleep with someone else.<br /><br />It's an intoxicating read. For the continuing presence of sex in the narrative, this is not a romance. It's mentioned, even described, but it's a vehicle by which the human characters are able to find their way around a city where they seem to be meant to be.<br /><br />Like The Orphan's Tales duology, this book is lush with words. Valente paints pictures with her prose - prose that's almost poetry, but not quite. The sights, sounds, and tastes are vivid and linger after I've shut the book. It's a seductive world where dreams end up being more real than waking.</span>

<div id="review_106701967" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/106701967" />
    <a title="Sandi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/811687-sandi"><img alt="Sandi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404709147p2/811687.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/106701967?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sandi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sandi" href="/user/show/811687-sandi">Sandi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/811687-sandi?shelf=2010">2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/811687-sandi?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/811687-sandi?shelf=hugo-nominees-2010">hugo-nominees-2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer106701967" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14430469496558222475"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3973532.Palimpsest' title='Palimpsest by Catherynne M. Valente' rel='nofollow noopener'>Palimpsest</a> is one seriously weird book. Consider the concept: If you have sex with someone with a map tattoo, you will "dream" that you are in the city of Palimpsest. Palimpsest is a really f'd up place, but people are desperate to go back. They are so desperate to go back and visit other parts of the city that they will have sex with anybody who has part of the map tattooed on them, sexual attraction and orientation have nothing to do with anything. <br/><br/>While there are a lot of sexual encounters in</span>
  <span id="freeText14430469496558222475" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3973532.Palimpsest" title="Palimpsest by Catherynne M. Valente" rel="nofollow noopener">Palimpsest</a> is one seriously weird book. Consider the concept: If you have sex with someone with a map tattoo, you will "dream" that you are in the city of Palimpsest. Palimpsest is a really f'd up place, but people are desperate to go back. They are so desperate to go back and visit other parts of the city that they will have sex with anybody who has part of the map tattooed on them, sexual attraction and orientation have nothing to do with anything. <br /><br />While there are a lot of sexual encounters in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3973532.Palimpsest" title="Palimpsest by Catherynne M. Valente" rel="nofollow noopener">Palimpsest</a>, none of it is erotic. It's quick and desperate. Each encounter leaves the characters and the reader emptier than the last. The characters aren't even attracted to the people they have sex with. It's especially uncomfortable when heterosexual characters are having homosexual encounters that they don't want but need so they can return to Palimpsest.<br /><br />The city of Palimpsest itself is surreal, like a dream or a nightmare. It's full of improbable people, impossible places and undefinable rules. It can only be reached by way of post-coital dreams unless you find the secret way to become a citizen. <br /><br />I give this book three stars because I'm not sure if I liked it or not. The writing is evocative and poetic. It captures the surreal quality of dreams perfectly. However, the desperation got to me. It was depressing and I never did quite figure out why the characters wanted to be a part of Palimpsest so much. It was like a drug and the story had a psychedelic quality to it that didn't quite appeal to me. However, I do give Valente points for creating something that was unlike anything I have ever read. <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3973532.Palimpsest" title="Palimpsest by Catherynne M. Valente" rel="nofollow noopener">Palimpsest</a> is a Hugo Award nominee this year, but I'm not really sure why. It strikes me more as psychedelia or surrealism than SF&amp;F. I suppose if you assume that the city of Palimpsest is real and that the map tattoos and everything that happens in the city are magic, then it might be borderline fantasy. I just didn't interpret it that way.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14430469496558222475" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_106337847" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/106337847" />
    <a title="Althea Ann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3846743-althea-ann"><img alt="Althea Ann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1411566466p2/3846743.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/106337847?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Althea Ann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Althea Ann" href="/user/show/3846743-althea-ann">Althea Ann</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer106337847" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1215645442308809583">My book club selection for the month.<br/>I'd heard Valente described as a steampunk author, but I really<br/>wouldn't classify this as being in that genre. I've yet to acquire her<br/>other books, but I'm on the lookout for them!<br/>Outside of our reality, there is a city called Palimpsest. Those who<br/>have visited the city mysteriously acquire a tattoo-like mark<br/>somewhere on their skin - and an inexplicable desire, almost an<br/>addiction, driving them to return. The only way the city can be<br/>entered is through sex with an</span>
  <span id="freeText1215645442308809583" style="display:none">My book club selection for the month.<br />I'd heard Valente described as a steampunk author, but I really<br />wouldn't classify this as being in that genre. I've yet to acquire her<br />other books, but I'm on the lookout for them!<br />Outside of our reality, there is a city called Palimpsest. Those who<br />have visited the city mysteriously acquire a tattoo-like mark<br />somewhere on their skin - and an inexplicable desire, almost an<br />addiction, driving them to return. The only way the city can be<br />entered is through sex with another traveller who bears the mark on<br />their body. The travellers to the city spend their time there<br />obsessively searching for a way to stay - something unknown to any<br />visitor, unheard-of by the natives, but rumored to exist.<br />Four people who arrived together in Palimpsest theorize that a<br />permanent entrance could be found if they find each other and meet in<br />the "real world," and they seek to do so...<br />The book is beautifully written, but definitely disturbing and<br />grotesque. Rich with details and odd obsessions, Valente captures the<br />feeling of bad dreams that are not quite nightmares - those dreams<br />that leave you with an unpleasant feeling for the day, but are filled<br />with fascinating and out-of-place elements that one can't stop<br />thinking of. The contradiction in the book is that for all its quirks<br />and oddities, Palimpsest is a curiously 'empty' place, devoid of the<br />richness of a real world. It is a dream. It is never fully (or, to me,<br />convincingly) explained why anyone would want to go there, let alone<br />stay there... but then again, I don't understand that about other<br />addictions, either, and the four characters are definitely credible<br />candidates for falling victim to such an escape: Oleg, an immigrant<br />locksmith without social ties, obsessed with his dead sister. Ludovic:<br />a bookbinder whose wife has left him because he cared for his books<br />more than her. November: a beekeeper whose bees are everything to her.<br />Sei: a woman from Japan who spends all her time riding trains. They<br />all believe that they will find what they lack in Palimpsest...<br />Not always a pleasant experience, but worth reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1215645442308809583" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3080893-sandra?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3080893-sandra?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer120186463" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9414948499086213793">I have to think about this one a while. What a weird experience.<br/><br/>9/21 - after mulling a while, I decided on 3 stars because I both loved and hated it. I decided I had to read it almost stream of consciousness as it's written, since stopping to ponder the words detracted from the mood. At times I was annoyed, irritated, aggravated by the characters. At other times I felt an almost unbearable soaring of spirit and longing along with the characters. At times I almost stopped reading wondering why I </span>
  <span id="freeText9414948499086213793" style="display:none">I have to think about this one a while. What a weird experience.<br /><br />9/21 - after mulling a while, I decided on 3 stars because I both loved and hated it. I decided I had to read it almost stream of consciousness as it's written, since stopping to ponder the words detracted from the mood. At times I was annoyed, irritated, aggravated by the characters. At other times I felt an almost unbearable soaring of spirit and longing along with the characters. At times I almost stopped reading wondering why I was subjecting myself to such misery; at other times I couldn't stop reading I was so enthralled.<br /><br />I'm totally mystified as to why this was in the running for a Hugo award. Isn't that an award for science fiction? I don't think this book is science fiction. I keep trying to decide what it is and think it's some sort of poetic metaphor for following one's bliss, for finding where passion takes one, for seeking one's meaning in unusual choices that are definitely against the norm.<br /><br />The author most definitely has a gift for using words almost as paint brushes or as musical instruments to create a mood larger than the meaning of the sentence or paragraph. It's worth a read if only to experience this strangely wonderful phenomenon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9414948499086213793" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_319151625" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/319151625" />
    <a title="Tracy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5524591-tracy"><img alt="Tracy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605029877p2/5524591.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/319151625?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tracy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tracy" href="/user/show/5524591-tracy">Tracy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5524591-tracy?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5524591-tracy?shelf=beautiful-and-indescribable">beautiful-and-indescribable</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5524591-tracy?shelf=adult-fiction">adult-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5524591-tracy?shelf=library-books">library-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer319151625" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10523955076929159542"><br/><br/> I finally finished this. I've been reading a little here and a little there for weeks and I finally finished this. I think it took me so long to read for the reasons Nataliya described in her review, the book alternately pulls you in and pushes you away. It is so filled with loss at times it was unbearable to go on. But I'm glad I did. For one thing the writing in this book is exquisite. Catherine Valente is a poet and it shows:<br/><br/>"She had dreamed heavily and the dream clung to her still-November </span>
  <span id="freeText10523955076929159542" style="display:none"><br /><br /> I finally finished this. I've been reading a little here and a little there for weeks and I finally finished this. I think it took me so long to read for the reasons Nataliya described in her review, the book alternately pulls you in and pushes you away. It is so filled with loss at times it was unbearable to go on. But I'm glad I did. For one thing the writing in this book is exquisite. Catherine Valente is a poet and it shows:<br /><br />"She had dreamed heavily and the dream clung to her still-November had always been a prodigy of dreams. Her father, a librarian, had made her write them down, and perhaps this had been the beginning of the slim brown books which now numbered so very many. She dreamed in color, more than color, in shades of gold and scarlet impossible in the waking world. She dreamed in languages she did and did not know, she dreamed strange and wonderful faces, narratives of recursive complexity, and her recall was meticulous, detailed, perfect as a list."<br /><br />"She often felt that she chased the ideal cup of coffee in her mind from table to table, the rich, thick, creamy coffee, spicy, bittersweet, that betrayed no hint of thinness or chemical flavoring, nothing less than total, fathomless devotion to the state of being itself. Every morning she pulled a delicate cup from its brass hook and filled it, hoping that it would be dark and deep and secret as a forest, and each morning it cooled too fast, had too much milk, stained the cup, made her nervous."<br /><br />And that is the author writing prosaically about every day things...her descriptions of Palimpsest itself are fantastic, gorgeous. Palimpsest is a city, a sexually transmitted city. You fuck someone who has been there, who bears the tattoo of it's streets somewhere on their body and you awaken in Palimpsest. When you fall asleep again you wake up back in this world with the tattoo somewhere on your body. The next person you fuck (if they haven't already been there) catches the city and the tattoo from you. It's like a drug, you are addicted. You become desperate to go back. You will fuck anyone to go back. "But this is how you do it: through the body and into the world. You fuck; you travel. That sounds crude, and you know usually it is. It's usually ugly, and fat, and sweaty, and lonely. Luckily, it's also usually quick. But afterwards ... we find a place where we belong."<br /><br />When you enter Palimpsest the first time you are bound to three strangers who have entered Palimpsest for the first time at the same instant as you. In order to emigrate in Palimpsest you have to find those three strangers and have sex with them, and enter Palimpsest together as a group. Some of these groups of four are splintered by the death of one of the four, some groups are destroyed because one of the four can't bear the city...it is too much, to beautiful, too colorful, too depraved. Some groups are never able to find each other and are so reduced to mindlessly coupling with anyone, anyone just to get back. The anguish of these lost souls trying to find their way back is like a tearing at the heart.<br /><br />This book isn't for everyone obviously. Some will object to the sex, others don't care for poetry. Some will find it to dark and too sad. But it is beautiful. I'd like to find some of Cathrynne Valente's poetry I think.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10523955076929159542" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_237421954" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/237421954" />
    <a title="Meg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6907372-meg"><img alt="Meg" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/237421954?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meg" href="/user/show/6907372-meg">Meg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6907372-meg?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer237421954" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9158945448984266434">To summarize: <br/><br/>Pros: Crocodile conga lines. Logophile’s dream. Rampant potential for “that’s what she said” jokes. Rampant potential for terrible puns. Barry Manilow. Euphemisms. So multicultural. Pirate frogs. Rum. Talking animals. Taking everything out of context. <br/><br/>Cons: Frog psychic wrapped in ragged fox fur - PETA cries, foxes die. Bugs. Gregor Samsa. Ear sex. No lols. Strange analogies. Train vagina visuals. Sexually Transmitted Tattoos. Elitism.<br/><br/><br/>Usually book cover summaries are so trite and b</span>
  <span id="freeText9158945448984266434" style="display:none">To summarize: <br><br>Pros: Crocodile conga lines. Logophile’s dream. Rampant potential for “that’s what she said” jokes. Rampant potential for terrible puns. Barry Manilow. Euphemisms. So multicultural. Pirate frogs. Rum. Talking animals. Taking everything out of context. <br><br>Cons: Frog psychic wrapped in ragged fox fur - PETA cries, foxes die. Bugs. Gregor Samsa. Ear sex. No lols. Strange analogies. Train vagina visuals. Sexually Transmitted Tattoos. Elitism.<br><br><br>Usually book cover summaries are so trite and boring that I only get about a sentence in before I abandon ship and just start reading the book instead. The summary of Catherynne Valente’s Palimpsest, however, was brought to my attention by a friend, and flat out wins the “I hope my friends, extended family and acquaintances don’t see me reading this” and “I’m too embarrassed to even check this out at the library because the librarian is going to think I’m a freak” summary award.<br><br>I won’t detract from the… unique summary by explicating the whole thing here. Instead, let me introduce you to my new favorite phrase from Catherynne (who shall henceforth be referred to as KitKat):<br><br> “Lyrically erotic.”<br><br>What, like Barry Manilow? <br><br>(Also, the phrase “lyrically erotic” makes me think of ear sex. I’m not sure what ear sex is. I am sure that I don’t want to know. Unless it involves Barry Manilow.)<br><br>Moving on.<br><br>I’d like to make a note here that if you’re not okay with debauchery and/or cannot stomach reading it, this book is not for you. Palimpsest is basically on a permanent bunny level of sexery.<br><br>Within the first page, the author uses big words to the point it seems like she wrote the book with a thesaurus at her side, or that she was one of those kids that almost won the Scripps National Spelling Bee and has a chip on her scapula and something to prove. I love it. (I also keep word lists. Multiple word lists. For funsies.) <br><br>The whole shebang starts off with a description of a factory and its workers. This actually ends up being pertinent later (much later). The author quickly goes off into a pretty-worded tangent. Rinse. Repeat. The description of the factory workers makes me have awesome visuals of crocodiles in a conga line with lunch pails. Read it yourself to find out why.<br><br>This is just the beginning of numerous visual imagery-laden scenes. KitKat loves her imagery. She rarely describes an entire scene, but instead narrows in on certain parts of it and then goes into minutiae. The rest is up to your imagination (which is great because my imagination is awesome).<br><br>There is a recurring scene involving a fortune telling frog in fox fur. (Is a frog wearing fur toadally wrong? At least it’s not wearing tadpoles!) Apparently, there’s some sort of freaky bonding thing that happens to whatever four people happen to sit in the frog’s “fortune chairs” with you, which are more or less the “entrance” to Palimpsest (I say more or less because there’s something else you have to do first). <br><br>I like to picture them sitting in something like this. But red.<br><br><span>[image error]</span><br><br>Also, the phrase “smells of sassafras and rum” was used in one of these little rendezvous, the latter half of which makes me think of Captain Jack. I now like to imagine the fox fur-wearing fortuneteller frog as fox fur-wearing fortuneteller pirate frog.<br><br>In case you haven’t guessed by now, animals can talk in Palimpsest. It’s like an R-rated Disney movie. Speaking of which, I’ve picked up another favorite phrase from a talking heron in the book, “I’ll trample you thoroughly, see if I won’t!”<br><br>There end up being four *semi-main characters – Sei, the blue-haired chick (trains really steam her engine); November, the beekeeper who likes to get beesy in Chinatown; Oleg, a man with a passion for locks (cue Louis XVI jokes), written by an author with a passion for making everything somehow sexual; and Lucia, the bookbinder’s wife. I can’t think of any good innuendo involving bookbinding right now, but you can go ahead and imagine that I did.<br><br>*I say “semi” because the book eventually ends up narrowing in on one particular character, though it’s pretty evenly split in the beginning of the book. Also, the main “character” of the novel arguably isn’t even a person at all.<br><br>They all get into Palimpsest by having sex with someone who’s already been and then going to the pirate frog fortuneteller. They must have more sex to get around the city. Everyone who has been to Palimpsest is marked with some sort of dark tattoo-like thing. I just visualize this, because it makes the whole thing more entertaining:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380363453i/714819.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>After you find all of this out, the book suddenly turns into a war novel, which is somehow connected to the factory, which somehow connects to one of the aforementioned main characters, who becomes the focal point of the story. I won’t ruin the surprise for you (even though I want to). Chaos (and sex aplenty) ensues. Interestingly, it also turns into an addiction novel, of sorts.<br><br>Basically, the book has all the mindfuckery of Hitchhiker’s Guide to the Galaxy with sex instead of funny, which leads us to the main shortcoming of the book – its relative lack of humor. Barely a joke was cracked. A few pithy side comments here and there, but no witticisms so great as to warrant you stealing it and telling your friends you came up with it yourself (you know you do it, too). <br><br>If I had to chose one word to describe this book: Sexcellent.<br><br>But really. While there were certain aspects of the book I found unnecessary if not leaning towards slightly distasteful, I cannot deny that this was an interesting read. <br><br>One thing that consistently irked me was the narrator breaking the fourth wall. It seemed trite, awkward, and unnecessary, and generally served to mar what was otherwise a very fluid novel.<br><br>On the other hand, KitKat has an incredible knack for anthropomorphism. In many cases, she quite literally humanizes animals and inanimate objects in a way that almost makes you feel more sympathetic to them then the actual human characters. She also manages to create a transient, not quite lucid, dream world which is every bit as ungraspable, ephemeral, and addictive to the reader as it is to the characters in the book. Despite all the horizontal limbo-ing going on with the main characters, the book still manages to be beautiful, in its own unique way. I would recommend it.<br></span>
  <a data-text-id="9158945448984266434" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_81774258" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/81774258" />
    <a title="Marley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72975-marley"><img alt="Marley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1527543658p2/72975.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/81774258?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marley" href="/user/show/72975-marley">Marley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer81774258" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8772255697359354076">This book is REALLY interesting, and a lot smuttier than I expected. The sense of all these people with a collective obsession that pierces the normal bounds of sexual decorum, and the way that such a bond DOESN'T necessarily mean they treat each other with understanding or warmth or kindness--now THAT is an interesting set of ideas. <br/><br/>Separately, this first book of Valente's I've read has some fascinating language. I definitely see what people are saying when they put her in the "really out there</span>
  <span id="freeText8772255697359354076" style="display:none">This book is REALLY interesting, and a lot smuttier than I expected. The sense of all these people with a collective obsession that pierces the normal bounds of sexual decorum, and the way that such a bond DOESN'T necessarily mean they treat each other with understanding or warmth or kindness--now THAT is an interesting set of ideas. <br /><br />Separately, this first book of Valente's I've read has some fascinating language. I definitely see what people are saying when they put her in the "really out there" portion of New Weird. Unlike a lot of lesser writers doing this stuff, her touches of the Decadents and catalogues of oddity are totally arresting, and lots of the book really does get by on the strength of its atmosphere and novelty.<br /><br />I'm going to leave this as a 4-star because of how much everyone seems to adore "The Orphan's Tales", but this book really sank its claws into me while I read it--on lunch breaks, between taking a shower and going out to meet waiting friends, in stolen moments that made me late to work. That's the kind of book this is, and I have a strong feeling I'm going to be reading everything of Valente's I run across.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8772255697359354076" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1399173472" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1399173472" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23841736-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532221628p2/23841736.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1399173472?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/23841736-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1399173472" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7835682174125731855">Do you guys know that I almost missed out on this wonder? Somewhere, and I can't find where, I saw it labeled as erotica and I have a very strong aversion to that genre. But then this came up in a book group and I do really love Valente's work, so I decided to give it a try.<br/><br/>This is a long way of wandering around the fact that I almost missed out not on a book, but an Absolutely Phenomenal Experience. I loved it. I loved every single second of this book. The prose is gorgeous, lush, and evocative</span>
  <span id="freeText7835682174125731855" style="display:none">Do you guys know that I almost missed out on this wonder? Somewhere, and I can't find where, I saw it labeled as erotica and I have a very strong aversion to that genre. But then this came up in a book group and I do really love Valente's work, so I decided to give it a try.<br /><br />This is a long way of wandering around the fact that I almost missed out not on a book, but an Absolutely Phenomenal Experience. I loved it. I loved every single second of this book. The prose is gorgeous, lush, and evocative. It drowns in sadness and loneliness and it makes you feel these four characters pain as though it were your own.<br /><br />And yes, sex is a part of it. The characters must have sex with someone who has a piece of the map in order to gain access to the addictive city of Palimpsest. And it's not the sex that's addictive, or even what happens once they get to the city, it's merely that they belong there. What would you do to end loneliness and despair? Would you have sex with a stranger? Any stranger, not just one you find attractive? Risk pregnancy or STDs? <br /><br />I love everything I've read by Valente, but this one is going into my Favorites folder. It just worked for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7835682174125731855" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_176757484" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/176757484" />
    <a title="Jacquelynn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5631943-jacquelynn"><img alt="Jacquelynn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308096170p2/5631943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/176757484?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacquelynn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacquelynn" href="/user/show/5631943-jacquelynn">Jacquelynn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer176757484" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14050393439803702324">Intriguing idea and beautiful prose that borders poetry. Also fascinating concept. However, I don't get much mileage from Palimpsest-there's just not much re-readability in it.<br/><br/>Valente's style of writing is dreamlike, floating on its own wing of metaphors and elaborate description. This is enhanced by (or exacerbated by, depending on the style of writing you enjoy) the presentation of scenes, which cuts in and out of each character's life, rather than coherently connecting them until later. By th</span>
  <span id="freeText14050393439803702324" style="display:none">Intriguing idea and beautiful prose that borders poetry. Also fascinating concept. However, I don't get much mileage from Palimpsest-there's just not much re-readability in it.<br /><br />Valente's style of writing is dreamlike, floating on its own wing of metaphors and elaborate description. This is enhanced by (or exacerbated by, depending on the style of writing you enjoy) the presentation of scenes, which cuts in and out of each character's life, rather than coherently connecting them until later. By the end, the novel has simply drifted away, and there's nothing in it that compels you to pick up the book again. As a first read, I would give it four stars, but...can I give something that rating when I can't enjoy the book more than once?<br /><br />Borrow it, don't buy it. However, be sure to listen to the music inspired by it: Quartered: Songs of Palimpsest by SJ Tucker. For better or worse, the music draws me into Palimpsest more than the book ever did. Those songs receive regular attendance from me. Alas, the same can't be said for the book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14050393439803702324" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4878104-kat?shelf=dude-where-is-the-plot">dude-where-is-the-plot</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4878104-kat?shelf=dark-and-urban-fantasy">dark-and-urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4878104-kat?shelf=unfortunately-abandoned">unfortunately-abandoned</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer313971093" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16375200739201217449"><i>"Sei pressed her cheek against the cold glass; strips of black mountains tore by under latern-blue clouds beyond her wide window. She knew a man was watching her - the way men on trains always watched her. The train car rocked gently from side to side, hushing its charges like a worried mother. She chewed on the ends of her dark blue hair. A stupid childhood habit, but Sei couldn't let it go. Her skin prickled as the man's eyes slid over her back."</i><br/><br/>The poetic prose is so beautiful that <i>Palimpsest</i></span>
  <span id="freeText16375200739201217449" style="display:none"><i>"Sei pressed her cheek against the cold glass; strips of black mountains tore by under latern-blue clouds beyond her wide window. She knew a man was watching her - the way men on trains always watched her. The train car rocked gently from side to side, hushing its charges like a worried mother. She chewed on the ends of her dark blue hair. A stupid childhood habit, but Sei couldn't let it go. Her skin prickled as the man's eyes slid over her back."</i><br /><br />The poetic prose is so beautiful that <i>Palimpsest's</i> lack of substance did not bother me for a long time. For a while, it felt like I was drifting around in one of those strange dreams you only manage to vaguely remember, because your alarm goes off in the middle of it. But when my alarm did go off, I realized that <i>Palimpsest</i> had no real plot, instead it kept me afloat because of Valente's lush, fascinating prose. Some of my dreams are like this; intriguing at first, but, eventually, dragging on aimlessly.</span>
